<div id="review_1361180979" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1361180979" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1361180979?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782010139345" href="/book/show/1519560.L_cume_des_jours">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=desert-island">desert-island</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=je-suis-snob">je-suis-snob</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=mandatory-reading">mandatory-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=used-bookstore-finds">used-bookstore-finds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=stole-it-from-mom">stole-it-from-mom</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=ouch-my-feels">ouch-my-feels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1361180979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13213843110063443075">This is a book I have been re-reading periodically for the past fifteen years. I had my mother’s battered old copy for the longest time, it got misplaced in a move and I had to get a shiny new copy immediately. Boris Vian wrote a beautiful, sexy, tragic love story – and he did it with the most enchanting, surrealist prose one can imagine without ever becoming nauseatingly adorable.<br/><br/>The imaginary Paris the characters live in doesn’t know gravity, clouds talk, sunrays can be plucked like guitar str</span>
  <span id="freeText13213843110063443075" style="display:none">This is a book I have been re-reading periodically for the past fifteen years. I had my mother’s battered old copy for the longest time, it got misplaced in a move and I had to get a shiny new copy immediately. Boris Vian wrote a beautiful, sexy, tragic love story – and he did it with the most enchanting, surrealist prose one can imagine without ever becoming nauseatingly adorable.<br /><br />The imaginary Paris the characters live in doesn’t know gravity, clouds talk, sunrays can be plucked like guitar strings, philosophers are superstars and you can fish eel in your kitchen sink. But when people are ill, rooms shrink and windows will not let sunlight in. The characters are candid and a little vain: the world is their oyster and they will gorge themselves on refined food, elegant clothes, Jazz and fresh love. Colin loves Chloé and Chick loves Alise. It would be so easy if there wasn’t money to waste on books, an extravagant wedding or doctors… It would be so easy if these people did not have to work and could simply love each other all day…<br /><br />(Most romantic quote of all time, in my opinion: "Qu'est-ce que vous faites dans la vie, vous? - J'apprends des choses, dit Colin. Et j'aime Chloé.")<br /><br />Vian created a world of wonders to show us the incredible beauty of falling in love in sharp contrast with the gritty realities of loss and grief. The freshness and carelessness of youth faced with the unpleasant realities of adult life. The original title translates literally into “The foam (or froth) of days”, and that hints at the superficiality of the story: the beauty on the surface that shines brightly in the sunlight, hiding the murky, scary depths of the ocean. This only makes the read more heartbreaking because we all know the bitter taste of growing up, the fear of the unknown we have to face when we shed our teenage skin and realize the world is not the enchanted place we had in mind.<br /><br />The prose in stunning and rather complex. Vian uses word plays, metaphors, similes and other tricks of language to create magical images and concepts that are more dream than reality. It takes a bit of getting used to, but as soon as your brain gets used to it, you will get sucked into this bizarre poetry. It can’t be described and really must be experienced to be understood.<br /><br />This is a lush, erotic and blood-stained little book that I recommend to everyone. But be warned, it can crack your heart like an egg and you will find yourself feeling the sharp sting of that ending for a long time after you close the book. Also, I want a pianocktail.<br /><br />(I’ve only ever read this book in French, so I cannot recommend a good English translation, sadly. Also, I recently watched the movie adaptation, easily found under the title "Mood Indigo". I liked it, but it was just a little too twee for my taste. I also can't quite believe Romain Duris and Audrey Tautou as Colin and Chloé, mostly because the characters always felt to me like they were barely 20 years old, and those actors are now gently pushing 40. It was still a sweet little film, and fans of the book should like it.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13213843110063443075" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2829831930" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2829831930" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2829831930?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780704300620" href="/book/show/3132189-froth-on-the-daydream">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=list-le-monde">list-le-monde</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=4-europe">4-europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2829831930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16662442254333882856">More like 3.5 stars. It has a kind of Disney reality in which animals, for example, talk to people and there is that somewhat infantile humor. The book begins in a kind of innocent world of some young people who haven't come across the suffering yet. The characters resist and are afraid of things that are for grown-ups - especially having to earn a living. The girls want to buy pretty things and boys want to be able to buy those things for them. As the suffering in its countless forms raises the</span>
  <span id="freeText16662442254333882856" style="display:none">More like 3.5 stars. It has a kind of Disney reality in which animals, for example, talk to people and there is that somewhat infantile humor. The book begins in a kind of innocent world of some young people who haven't come across the suffering yet. The characters resist and are afraid of things that are for grown-ups - especially having to earn a living. The girls want to buy pretty things and boys want to be able to buy those things for them. As the suffering in its countless forms raises the ugly hand for the four youngsters that are central characters of the book, they struggle to keep the happiness they had gained in their innocent times. <br /><br />The surreal art, as far as I understand, tries to use elements from the unconscious mind, and since 'meaning' is an invention of the consious mind - everything surreal must be definition be meaningless or at least have a meaning that is very difficult to put in words. I am not sure surrealism is the word for strange occurrences of the book. They are more like literary devices used by the author to color his prose n the same innocent shade with which its main characters are made. Even as the characters themselves struggle against the suffering of the world, the prose continues to retain the sam Disney shade till the very end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16662442254333882856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614339-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21278688?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/614339-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of Master and Margarita</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21278688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3073433038035087630">In a nutshell, L'Ecume Des Jours reads like an Edith Wharton novel set in Pepperland (sorry, non-cinephiles for the obscure reference). The book rewires your brain after the first 30 pages; if it doesn't, this book is not for you. Themes/motifs: class struggle, love, flowers, Sartre, mice, death and cocktails. My favorite image from the book is when the protagonist loses his fortune and takes a job at a weapons factory. You see, though, the rifle barrels are grown from seeds that must be warmed </span>
  <span id="freeText3073433038035087630" style="display:none">In a nutshell, L'Ecume Des Jours reads like an Edith Wharton novel set in Pepperland (sorry, non-cinephiles for the obscure reference). The book rewires your brain after the first 30 pages; if it doesn't, this book is not for you. Themes/motifs: class struggle, love, flowers, Sartre, mice, death and cocktails. My favorite image from the book is when the protagonist loses his fortune and takes a job at a weapons factory. You see, though, the rifle barrels are grown from seeds that must be warmed at 98.6 degrees. You guessed it. Someone has to lie on top of the seeds so they properly germinate. Colin's love for his dying wife, Chloe (who has a waterlily growing in her lung), leads to a bad crop of rifle barrels: they all have a steel flower growing from their tips.</span>

<div id="review_1486623823" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1486623823" />
    <a title="Borum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15410264-borum"><img alt="Borum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562163411p2/15410264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1486623823?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Borum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Borum" href="/user/show/15410264-borum">Borum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374534226" href="/book/show/17332376-mood-indigo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1486623823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8681373023963194870">Wow. This novel started off as one of those dreamy paintings of Marc Chagall where the people seem to take wings and float up in the rainbow and marzipan-scented air just to land a kiss on the lover's cheek. But then, after the ominous shortcut through the copper mine, this turns into a grotesquely surreal painting reminiscent of Dali. It had its mildly sharp witted stabs at the capitalist society in the beginning but in the end it becomes as inhumane as a butcher's knife slicing everyone into a</span>
  <span id="freeText8681373023963194870" style="display:none">Wow. This novel started off as one of those dreamy paintings of Marc Chagall where the people seem to take wings and float up in the rainbow and marzipan-scented air just to land a kiss on the lover's cheek. But then, after the ominous shortcut through the copper mine, this turns into a grotesquely surreal painting reminiscent of Dali. It had its mildly sharp witted stabs at the capitalist society in the beginning but in the end it becomes as inhumane as a butcher's knife slicing everyone into a pile of flesh like a painting of Francis Bacon.<br />Like the rose on the missile rejected and stained with blood, the romantic poetry becomes sullen with reality that is even more surreal than the fantasy of love. <br />I have been wondering why the title of the movie became Mood Indigo. Indigo is the deepest blue and Mood Indigo is Duke Ellington's jazz standard. On reading the book I felt the jazzy beat that pulse throughout the pianocktail and biglemoi but ended up feeling the blues hidden beneath the luxurious facade. It's extremely difficult to explain this synesthetic and surreal work in mere words. I think it would be better to express it in the taste or fragrance of a fantastic cocktail with a bitter aftertaste that may leave a terrible hangover afterwards.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8681373023963194870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1895636423" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1895636423" />
    <a title="Aravindakshan Narasimhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62878026-aravindakshan-narasimhan"><img alt="Aravindakshan Narasimhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600662048p2/62878026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1895636423?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aravindakshan Narasimhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aravindakshan Narasimhan" href="/user/show/62878026-aravindakshan-narasimhan">Aravindakshan Narasimhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1895636423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7621627032963575478">Imagine you are sitting in a restaurant on a sultry evening where the trees are protesting not to dance to the tune of nature, and birds do not show their face or call out. You have started your eating adventure with much hope, only to be shattered by the poor reception and ergonomical errors on the side of hotel management, weary faces of servers, unhindered cacophony and to top it all, a poor food. All makes it one nightmarish dining experience already accentuated by your boring day at work. S</span>
  <span id="freeText7621627032963575478" style="display:none">Imagine you are sitting in a restaurant on a sultry evening where the trees are protesting not to dance to the tune of nature, and birds do not show their face or call out. You have started your eating adventure with much hope, only to be shattered by the poor reception and ergonomical errors on the side of hotel management, weary faces of servers, unhindered cacophony and to top it all, a poor food. All makes it one nightmarish dining experience already accentuated by your boring day at work. So when you are almost restless to get out of this nightmare, without you being aware, u order an ice cream.<br /><br />You take the spoon absentmindedly. The lavish and bricolage of colours that try appeasing you through their glass encase does not bring you back to present. You seem to have inserted your spoon inside your mouth now. Just at the moment when it starts to embrace your tongue fully and play with your saliva, you suddenly wake from your slumber. Like a bucket full of ice water that has been made to dance on your face, your eyes turn reddish and you are finally aware that you are sitting inside a restaurant and eating an ice cream!<br /><br />You without giving much thought to its physical charm, pierce the spoon into its toppings and not getting much deeper, curve it up and rise it to your mouth in a momentarily attained bourgeois fashion to eat another of this supposedly heavenly mixture.<br /><br />Holy smokes!! You are sent a mild ride around clouds and rainbows, and an extended play of orgasmic sensations pervades you and is visible on your face, of whose quality can only be initiated by pure arts!<br /><br />Just when you are about to plunge into the last part and main part, you feel as if you are biting something sour, as sour as it can get. But, this anti-villain, a wrong note in a culinary masterpiece has left a lasting sensation through it's wicked charm on u, and contrary to what should have happened, you keep tasting it to finish it, not out of obligation, but beyond human understanding. Like few peoples' addiction for souring quality in lemon.<br /> <br />You finish it with a sympathetic feeling for the toppings which had covered this dark wicked pieces.<br />Still the taste lingers, and you yet to recover from it and come back to your senses. You walk out only to run back to the menu card to find the name of the ice cream u had just enjoyed and you tip the server who nods happily. You walk out with such indescribable joy that you had never had felt before.<br /><br />This is how it was reading Boris Vian's "Froth on the daydream"<br /><br />This is not like other books where you walk into a store with your headphones adorning your ears and neck, and you casually take out your favorite Author's latest bestseller, or one that has been suggested by your so called "bibliophile" friend, which you can flaunt it to your like minded friends later. No! This is purely a serendipitous act, one sent by holy god to you. Probably the cover may have attracted you, or the title, or the description, or synopsis, or a review may have interested you.<br /><br />It may most certainly going to be there lying in your shelf ,without being touched by you post your buying. Someday you do take it out to see what this could offer, only to be blown away completely for it's various swing in moods, words, and imagery.<br /><br />But that is how this book has lived and will live. Such is its bittersweet fate is.<br /><br />Anyway, the book takes a usual love story --- people meeting accidentally with minor connections and falling in love at first sight. Vian for the most part has hinted at a fairy land setting with characters living like they pass their time at beaches. Characters are near perfect sketches of ideal humans without any slight cynicism, like innocent cute little dainties - naive and pure.<br /><br />He makes inanimate objects to take life by responding to characters mood - like a shrinking villa that responds to the mood of the character, or mouse acting like humans do.<br /><br />What makes this usual love story fresh and interesting is the fact that, Vian knows how to play with words, to create powerful imagery while throwing in a dash of light humour.<br /><br />The tone is muffled even when he could have played it more emotionally. <br />But, we feel for the characters because they are so genuine , that I found comparing them to Ozu's unbelievable believable characters.<br /><br />Novel takes a 180 degree turn in the last quarter; when finally those hanging in the air had to land to face the ground reality and cope with it, but only to be consumed by fate finally.<br /><br />It ends in a most bizzare fashion, that was reminiscent of Bergman's Seventh Seal climax, for its vivid visual description or the Yugoslavian black-wave marriage film "Lisice", for its mood and emotion, when the characters hike a hill when one of their guys has left them.<br /><br />Simple fact that there are more wonderful things that one can find in the novel, like the marvellous creation of Vian called "Pianococktail" which works in its own mechanism- where point of music and drinks flavors marry, makes it more deserving read than it has been bestowed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7621627032963575478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Still, surprisingly enjoyable!!<br />And ho man, that whole ending that tried to explain some things, but instead furthers messes you up.... <span id="freeTextContainer12629825784553028849">This novel starts out innocently with light-hearted love to the tune of Duke Ellington. Then the blood starts to flow; people are crushed, mice commit suicide, houses collapse in mourning and decay, and the surrealism turns from romantic whimsy to absurd tragedy. References to Jazz music set the tone, which is the most absorbing aspect of the novel for me. Everything is about ambiance: there's no Bildungsroman character development, because the characters are just setting, just mood. Their metam</span>
  <span id="freeText12629825784553028849" style="display:none">This novel starts out innocently with light-hearted love to the tune of Duke Ellington. Then the blood starts to flow; people are crushed, mice commit suicide, houses collapse in mourning and decay, and the surrealism turns from romantic whimsy to absurd tragedy. References to Jazz music set the tone, which is the most absorbing aspect of the novel for me. Everything is about ambiance: there's no Bildungsroman character development, because the characters are just setting, just mood. Their metamorphosis in love and death is mirrored by the transformation of the physical surroundings. As the water lilies grow in Chloe's lungs, the breathing space in the apartment also closes in on itself: windows shrink, sunlight recedes in solemn solstice, the mouse with black whiskers escapes just before the floor meets the ceiling. And when things break down, they break all the way down. </span>

<div id="review_1516296533" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1516296533" />
    <a title="Akemi G." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28637268-akemi-g"><img alt="Akemi G." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442980095p2/28637268.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1516296533?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Akemi G." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Akemi G." href="/user/show/28637268-akemi-g">Akemi G.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780374534226" href="/book/show/17332376-mood-indigo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28637268-akemi-g?shelf=read-fiction">read-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1516296533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12984444968003566480">Some people say there are too many love stories already. I disagree. There aren't enough love stories that really get you feel that sweet-yet-innately-sad but-oh-so-beautiful feelings. This French novel, <i>L'Écume des Jours</i>, originally published in 1947, is such a rare jewel. I gather it has been translated into English three times with different titles:<br/>* Froth On the Daydream (1967)<br/>* Mood Indigo (1979)<br/>* Foam of the Daze (2012)<br/>I wanted to get the newest translation, but I could only find the secon</span>
  <span id="freeText12984444968003566480" style="display:none">Some people say there are too many love stories already. I disagree. There aren't enough love stories that really get you feel that sweet-yet-innately-sad but-oh-so-beautiful feelings. This French novel, <i>L'Écume des Jours</i>, originally published in 1947, is such a rare jewel. I gather it has been translated into English three times with different titles:<br />* Froth On the Daydream (1967)<br />* Mood Indigo (1979)<br />* Foam of the Daze (2012)<br />I wanted to get the newest translation, but I could only find the second one at my bookstore (Powell's). It's the version that is taking liberty in title, which made me hesitate, but from the back cover, I gather the translator, Stanley Chapman, knew Boris Vian personally, so I decided to give it a try. <br /><br />The story is simple--to some extent, it's like Erick Segal's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/73968.Love_Story__Love_Story___1_" title="Love Story (Love Story, #1) by Erich Segal" rel="nofollow noopener">Love Story</a>. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[A boy meets a girl. He falls in love and marries her. She becomes sick and dies. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Vian presents another couple, who are equally crazy in love, as a contrast. <br /><br />What makes this simple story shine and saves it from its helpless tragedy is the liberal amount of plays of words and images. <br /><br />The word plays vary from puns that might appear like typos ("the elm of eternity"? -- oh, you mean "the realm of eternity"), made-up words ("ephemerald amethystle clockwork ring"), and more, often sounding almost ridiculous. The plays of images and ideas -- what shall I choose as an example--the boy has invented a piano which mixes drinks that lets you taste the music. <br /><br />Here is a passage how he was just before he meets her:<br /><blockquote>His heart swelled up to ten times its normal size, became completely weightless, lifted him up above the earth, and he went straight in after them.</blockquote><br /><br />I guess this book chooses its readers. If you've been in love, and if you can appreciate expressions that is so unreal that is true, you'll enjoy it. I like what the Foreword says:<br /><blockquote>... Only two things really matter--there's love, every kind of love, with every kind of pretty girl; and there's the music of Duke Ellington ... the few pages which follow as an illustration of this draw their entire strength from the fact that the story is completely true since I made it up from beginning to end. ... a projection of reality, under favorable circumstances, on to an irregularly tilting, and consequently distorting plane of reference.</blockquote> <br /><br /><i>A note on translation</i><br />So I've only read Chapman translation; I haven't checked other English translations and I don't read French. And I love this translation. I also love this does NOT come with footnotes. I just enjoyed the delightful words. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12984444968003566480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1761575216" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1761575216" />
    <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant"><img alt="Stephen Durrant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362701086p2/1457001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1761575216?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Durrant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Durrant" href="/user/show/1457001-stephen-durrant">Stephen Durrant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22261379-mood-indigo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1761575216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1844742121942315501">If you come to this charming but tragic novel, as I did, from Le Monde’s list of the one hundred greatest books of the twentieth century, you will most likely be disappointed. It ranks number ten on that list—and that includes ALL books, not just novels (poetry, essays, philosophy, etc.); and that’s the WHOLE world, not just France! Okay, time for a tangential diatribe. I love France and spend several months every year there. I deeply appreciate the French love of literature and the fact that a </span>
  <span id="freeText1844742121942315501" style="display:none">If you come to this charming but tragic novel, as I did, from Le Monde’s list of the one hundred greatest books of the twentieth century, you will most likely be disappointed. It ranks number ten on that list—and that includes ALL books, not just novels (poetry, essays, philosophy, etc.); and that’s the WHOLE world, not just France! Okay, time for a tangential diatribe. I love France and spend several months every year there. I deeply appreciate the French love of literature and the fact that a first-rate bookstore and a highly literate and opinionated reader are never far away. And, yes, one always expects a good dose of parochialism from lists like this. Still, does France, and the Francophone world, really deserve six of the top ten, twelve of the top twenty, and forty-eight of the top one hundred? Meanwhile, Germany manages to have eight books on the list, Italy six, and the whole Spanish-speaking world only three! Moreover, I would think a list of the greatest works of the twentieth century would at least give a polite, if condescending, nod to Asia. Nope. Tanizaki, Soseki, Kawabata, Lu Xun, and company are all passed over in silence. Yes, in case you hadn’t noticed, a segment of the Western intelligentsia is steadfastly and unabashedly Eurocentric. Okay, end of diatribe. Boris Vian’s 1947 “L’Ecume des jours,” re-named in this English translation as “Mood Indigo,” takes us into a surrealist world, much akin to what is later called “magical realism.” Mice talk, a character falls ill from a water-lily blooming in her lung, eels come out of water taps, and descriptions like the following proliferate: “On one side of the road the wind was blowing and on the other side it was not. You could take your choice of which side you liked best. Every other tree gave shade, and in the ditch on one side only could there be found frogs” (80). My favorite character in the novel is Chick, who has a self-destructive obsession with a prolific and charismatic philosopher named Jean-Pulse Heartre. Chick spends himself into poverty trying to obtain a copy of every word Jean-Pulse has written as well as Heartre’s old clothes and trademark pipe. The central characters in this story, however, are Colin and Chloe, whose flower-laden and ultimately tragic love affair alternately provokes smiles and tears. Very well done, Mr. Vian. A good read. Does it deserve its top-ten ranking? No. Top one hundred. Probably not. Better than Tanizaki’s “Makioka Sisters” or Soseki’s “Kokoro”? Not IMHO, but they were written on a distant island far removed from what Pascale Casanova labels “The World Republic of Letters” (i.e., France).</span>
  <a data-text-id="1844742121942315501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Pour l\'écrire, l\'auteur s\'est documenté auprès du docteur Charcot, célèbre spécialiste des névroses et des hystéries. Mais surtout, il s\'est probablement inspiré des troubles nerveux qui commençaient alors à le détruire et qui allaient bientôt l\'empoter. Sans doute est-ce la raison pour laquelle cette histoire, qui évoque la possession de son unique personnage par une puissance invisible, est racontée sans aucun des artifices habituels de la fiction, mais avec une sécheresse presque clinique. À cet égard Le Horla rompt radicalement avec les traditions romantiques ou symboliste, et ressorti à ce que l\'on pourrait appeler un &quot;Fantastique objectif&quot;, voire &quot;naturaliste&quot;. Ce recueil contient une autre nouvelle fantastique. L\'Auberge, et des récits procédant de la veine réaliste habituelle de leur auteur, avec cet impitoyable mélange de cruauté psychologique et de pessimisme social qui caractérise l\'ensemble de son Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his admirers as Bel-Ami, is offered a job as a journalist on La Vie francaise and soon makes a great success of his new career. But he also comes face to face with the realities of the corrupt society in which he lives - the sleazy colleagues, the manipulative mistresses, and wily financiers - and swiftly learns to become an arch-seducer, blackmailer and social climber in a world where love is only a means to an end. Written when Maupassant was at the height of his powers, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is a novel of great frankness and cynicism, but it is also infused with the sheer joy of life - depicting the scenes and characters of Paris in the belle epoque with wit, sensitivity, and humanity. Douglas Parmee\'s translation captures all the vigour and vitality of Maupassant\'s novel. His introduction explores the similarities between Bel-Ami and Maupassant himself and demonstrates the skill with which the author depicts his large cast of characters and the French society of the <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4641998597668189333">Boris Vian was a French polymath: writer, poet, musician, singer, translator, critic, actor, inventor and engineer. He is best remembered for novels such as L’Écume des jours and L'Arrache-cœur (translated into English as Froth on the Daydream and Heartsnatcher, respectively). He is also known for highly controversial "criminal" fiction released under the pseudonym <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7024381.Vernon_Sullivan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Vernon Sullivan</a> and some of his </span>
  <span id="freeText4641998597668189333" style="display:none">Boris Vian was a French polymath: writer, poet, musician, singer, translator, critic, actor, inventor and engineer. He is best remembered for novels such as L’Écume des jours and L'Arrache-cœur (translated into English as Froth on the Daydream and Heartsnatcher, respectively). He is also known for highly controversial "criminal" fiction released under the pseudonym <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7024381.Vernon_Sullivan" rel="nofollow noopener">Vernon Sullivan</a> and some of his songs (particularly the anti-war Le Déserteur). Vian was also fascinated with jazz: he served as liaison for, among others, Duke Ellington and Miles Davis in Paris, wrote for several French jazz-reviews (Le Jazz Hot, Paris Jazz) and published numerous articles dealing with jazz both in the United States and in France.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4641998597668189333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
